<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_eukaryotes">Origin of eukaryotes <span class="anchor" id="Eukaryogenesis"></span><span class="anchor" id="Origin_of_eukaryotes"></span><span class="anchor" id="Last_common_ancestor"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Origin of eukaryotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eocyte_hypothesis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Eocyte_hypothesis.png/310px-Eocyte_hypothesis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Eocyte_hypothesis.png/465px-Eocyte_hypothesis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Eocyte_hypothesis.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eocyte_hypothesis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The three-domains tree and the <a href="/wiki/Eocyte_hypothesis" title="Eocyte hypothesis">Eocyte hypothesis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="1122" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/310px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/465px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/620px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing a possible relationship between the eukaryotes and other forms of life;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> eukaryotes are colored red, <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> green and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> blue</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4800" data-file-width="8400" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png/310px-Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png/465px-Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png/620px-Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phylogenetic_Tree_of_Life.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eocyte tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tree_life_2016_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-tree_life_2016-99">[98]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The origin of the eukaryotic cell is a milestone in the evolution of life, since eukaryotes include all complex cells and almost all multicellular organisms. A number of approaches have been used to find the first eukaryote and their closest relatives. The <b>last eukaryotic common ancestor</b> (LECA) is the hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">last common ancestor</a> of all living eukaryotes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabaldón_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gabaldón-5">[5]</a></sup> and was most likely a <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">biological population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Malley_Leger_Wideman_Ruiz-Trillo_pp._338–344_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Malley_Leger_Wideman_Ruiz-Trillo_pp._338–344-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Eukaryotes have a number of features that differentiate them from prokaryotes, including an <a href="/wiki/Endomembrane_system" title="Endomembrane system">endomembrane system</a>, and unique biochemical pathways such as <a href="/wiki/Sterane" title="Sterane">sterane</a> synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sterane_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-sterane-101">[100]</a></sup> A set of proteins called eukaryotic signature proteins (ESPs) was proposed to identify eukaryotic relatives in 2002: They have no homology to proteins known in other domains of life by then, but they appear to be universal among eukaryotes. They include proteins that make up the cytoskeleton, the complex transcription machinery, membrane-sorting systems, the nuclear pore, as well as some enzymes in the biochemical pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11805300_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11805300-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossils">Fossils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fossils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The timing of this series of events is hard to determine; <a href="/wiki/Andrew_H._Knoll" title="Andrew H. Knoll">Knoll</a> (2006) suggests they developed approximately 1.6–2.1 billion years ago. Some <a href="/wiki/Acritarch" title="Acritarch">acritarchs</a> are known from at least 1.65 billion years ago, and the possible alga <i><a href="/wiki/Grypania" title="Grypania">Grypania</a></i> has been found as far back as 2.1 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Geosiphon" title="Geosiphon">Geosiphon</a></i>-like fossil <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Diskagma" title="Diskagma">Diskagma</a></i> has been found in paleosols 2.2 billion years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Organized living structures have been found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_shale" title="Black shale">black shales</a> of the Palaeoproterozoic Francevillian B Formation in Gabon, dated at 2.1 billion years old. Eukaryotic life could have evolved at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Fossils that are clearly related to modern groups start appearing an estimated 1.2 billion years ago, in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Red_algae#Fossil_record" title="Red algae">red algae</a>, though recent work suggests the existence of fossilized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filamentous_algae" title="Filamentous algae">filamentous algae</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vindhya" title="Vindhya">Vindhya</a> basin dating back perhaps to 1.6 to 1.7 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of eukaryotic-specific <a href="/wiki/Biomarker_(petroleum)" title="Biomarker (petroleum)">biomarkers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sterane" title="Sterane">steranes</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shales</a> previously indicated that eukaryotes were present in these rocks dated at 2.7 billion years old,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sterane_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-sterane-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> which was even 300 million years older than the first geological records of the appreciable amount of molecular oxygen during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxidation_Event" title="Great Oxidation Event">Great Oxidation Event</a>. However, these Archaean biomarkers were eventually rebutted as later contaminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Currently, putatively the oldest biomarker records are only ~800 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> In contrast, a molecular clock analysis suggests the emergence of sterol biosynthesis as early as 2.3 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> and thus there is a huge gap between molecular data and geological data, which hinders a reasonable inference of the eukaryotic evolution through biomarker records before 800 million years ago. The nature of steranes as eukaryotic biomarkers is further complicated by the production of sterols by some bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whenever their origins, eukaryotes may not have become ecologically dominant until much later; a massive uptick in the zinc composition of marine sediments <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=800">800</a> million years ago</span> has been attributed to the rise of substantial populations of eukaryotes, which preferentially consume and incorporate zinc relative to prokaryotes, approximately a billion years after their origin (at the latest).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29869832_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29869832-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2019, biologists reported that the very large <a href="/wiki/Medusavirus" title="Medusavirus">medusavirus</a>, or a relative, may have been responsible, at least in part, for the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary emergence</a> of complex eukaryotic cells from simpler <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotic cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30728258_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30728258-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_Archaea">Relationship to Archaea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Relationship to Archaea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The nuclear DNA and genetic machinery of eukaryotes is more similar to <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> than <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a>, leading to a controversial suggestion that eukaryotes should be grouped with Archaea in the clade <a href="/wiki/Neomura" title="Neomura">Neomura</a>. In other respects, such as membrane composition, eukaryotes are similar to Bacteria. Three main explanations for this have been proposed:
</p>
<ul><li>Eukaryotes resulted from the complete fusion of two or more cells, wherein the cytoplasm formed from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterium" title="Bacterium">bacterium</a>, and the nucleus from an archaeon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viral_Eukaryogenesis" title="Viral Eukaryogenesis">from a virus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> or from a <a href="/wiki/Pre-cell" title="Pre-cell">pre-cell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup></li>
<li>Eukaryotes developed from Archaea, and acquired their bacterial characteristics through the <a href="/wiki/Symbiogenesis" title="Symbiogenesis">endosymbiosis</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proto-mitochondrion" title="Proto-mitochondrion">proto-mitochondrion</a> of bacterial origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></li>
<li>Eukaryotes and Archaea developed separately from a modified bacterium.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Primordial_biogenesis.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="677" data-file-width="1223" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Primordial_biogenesis.svg/310px-Primordial_biogenesis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Primordial_biogenesis.svg/465px-Primordial_biogenesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Primordial_biogenesis.svg/620px-Primordial_biogenesis.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Primordial_biogenesis.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the origin of life with the Eukaryotes appearing early, not derived from Prokaryotes, as proposed by Richard Egel in 2012. This view implies that the UCA was relatively large and complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egel_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egel-121">[120]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Alternative proposals include:
</p>
<ul><li>The chronocyte hypothesis postulates that a primitive eukaryotic cell was formed by the endosymbiosis of both archaea and bacteria by a third type of cell, termed a <b>chronocyte</b>. This is mainly to account for the fact that eukaryotic signature proteins were not found anywhere else by 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11805300_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11805300-102">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li>The universal common ancestor (UCA) of the current tree of life was a complex organism that survived a mass extinction event rather than an early stage in the evolution of life. Eukaryotes and in particular akaryotes (Bacteria and Archaea) evolved through reductive loss, so that similarities result from differential retention of original features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HariTunlKurl13_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-HariTunlKurl13-122">[121]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Assuming no other group is involved, there are three possible phylogenies for the Bacteria, Archaea, and Eukaryota in which each is monophyletic. These are labelled 1 to 3 in the table below, with a modification of hypothesis 2 making the 4th column: The <i>eocyte hypothesis</i>, in which the Archaea are paraphyletic. (The table and the names for the hypotheses are based on Harish &amp; Kurland, 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harish17_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harish17-123">[122]</a></sup>)
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Alternative hypotheses for the base of the tree of life

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In recent years, most researchers have favoured either the three domains (3D) or the <a href="/wiki/Eocyte_hypothesis" title="Eocyte hypothesis">eocyte hypothesis</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">rRNA</a> analysis supports the eocyte scenario, apparently with the Eukaryote root in <a href="/wiki/Excavata" title="Excavata">Excavata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tree_life_2016_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-tree_life_2016-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cavalier-Smith2010b_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cavalier-Smith2010b-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cavalier-Smith2013_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cavalier-Smith2013-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cavalier2014_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-cavalier2014-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cavalier-Smith_2018_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cavalier-Smith_2018-84">[83]</a></sup> A cladogram supporting the eocyte hypothesis, positioning eukaryotes within Archaea, based on phylogenomic analyses of the <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(archaea)" title="Asgard (archaea)">Asgard</a> archaea, is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpangSaw2015_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpangSaw2015-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zaremba-NiedzwiedzkaCaceres2017_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zaremba-NiedzwiedzkaCaceres2017-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu_2018_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liu_2018-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r984819063" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
</p>
<table class="clade" style="font-size:80%; line-height:80%">

</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel"> 
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-label"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p> "<a href="/wiki/Thorarchaeota" title="Thorarchaeota">Thorarchaeota</a>"
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel"> 
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-label"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r984819063" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
<table class="clade">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="clade-label first"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heimdallarchaeota" title="Heimdallarchaeota">Heimdallarchaeota</a>"
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel"> 
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-label"> 
</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p> <b>Eukaryota</b><br/> (<small>+<a href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacteria" title="Alphaproteobacteria">Alphaproteobacteria</a></small>)
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><br/>
In this scenario, the Asgard group is seen as a sister taxon of the <a href="/wiki/TACK" title="TACK">TACK</a> group, which comprises <a href="/wiki/Thermoproteota" title="Thermoproteota">Thermoproteota</a> (formerly named <a href="/wiki/Eocyte_hypothesis" title="Eocyte hypothesis">eocytes</a> or Crenarchaeota), <a href="/wiki/Nitrososphaerota" title="Nitrososphaerota">Nitrososphaerota</a> (formerly Thaumarchaeota), and others. This group is reported contain many of the eukaryotic signature proteins and produce vesicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20200115_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20200115-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, there was significant pushback against this scenario, arguing that the eukaryotes did not emerge within the Archaea. Cunha <i>et al.</i> produced analyses supporting the three domains (3D) or Woese hypothesis (2 in the table above) and rejecting the eocyte hypothesis (4 above).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> Harish and Kurland found strong support for the earlier two empires (2D) or Mayr hypothesis (1 in the table above), based on analyses of the coding sequences of protein domains. They rejected the eocyte hypothesis as the least likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HariKurl17_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-HariKurl17-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harish17_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harish17-123">[122]</a></sup> A possible interpretation of their analysis is that the universal common ancestor (UCA) of the current tree of life was a complex organism that survived an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_bottleneck" title="Evolutionary bottleneck">evolutionary bottleneck</a>, rather than a simpler organism arising early in the history of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HariTunlKurl13_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-HariTunlKurl13-122">[121]</a></sup> On the other hand, the researchers who came up with Asgard re-affirmed their hypothesis with additional Asgard samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> Since then, the publication of additional Asgard archaeal genomes and the independent reconstruction of phylogenomic trees by multiple independent laboratories have provided additional support for an Asgard archaeal origin of eukaryotes.
</p><p>Details of the relation of Asgard archaea members and eukaryotes are still under consideration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31384702_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31384702-128">[127]</a></sup> although, in January 2020, scientists reported that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candidatus_Prometheoarchaeum_syntrophicum" title="Candidatus Prometheoarchaeum syntrophicum">Candidatus Prometheoarchaeum syntrophicum</a></i>, a type of cultured Asgard archaea, may be a possible link between simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a> and complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> microorganisms about two billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-2020115_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-2020115-129">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20200115_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20200115-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Endomembrane_system_and_mitochondria">Endomembrane system and mitochondria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Endomembrane system and mitochondria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The origins of the endomembrane system and mitochondria are also unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> The <b>phagotrophic hypothesis</b> proposes that eukaryotic-type membranes lacking a cell wall originated first, with the development of endocytosis, whereas mitochondria were acquired by ingestion as endosymbionts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> The <b>syntrophic hypothesis</b> proposes that the proto-eukaryote relied on the proto-mitochondrion for food, and so ultimately grew to surround it. Here the membranes originated after the engulfment of the mitochondrion, in part thanks to mitochondrial genes (the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_hypothesis" title="Hydrogen hypothesis">hydrogen hypothesis</a> is one particular version).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a study using genomes to construct <a href="/wiki/Supertree" title="Supertree">supertrees</a>, Pisani <i>et al.</i> (2007) suggest that, along with evidence that there was never a mitochondrion-less eukaryote, eukaryotes evolved from a <a href="/wiki/Syntrophy" title="Syntrophy">syntrophy</a> between an archaea closely related to <a href="/wiki/Thermoplasmatales" title="Thermoplasmatales">Thermoplasmatales</a> and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacterium" title="Alphaproteobacterium">alphaproteobacterium</a>, likely a <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a> driven by sulfur or hydrogen. The mitochondrion and its genome is a remnant of the alphaproteobacterial endosymbiont.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> The majority of the genes from the symbiont have been transferred to the nucleus. They make up most of the metabolic and energy-related pathways of the eukaryotic cell, while the information system (DNA polymerase, transcription, translation) is retained from archaea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypotheses">Hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a> have been proposed as to how eukaryotic cells came into existence. These hypotheses can be classified into two distinct classes – autogenous models and chimeric models.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Autogenous_models">Autogenous models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Autogenous models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062633282">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .content .thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){display:flex;justify-content:center;flex-wrap:wrap;align-content:flex-start;flex-direction:column}}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output div.thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){font-size:94%;text-align:center;overflow:hidden;min-width:100px}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output div.thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){margin:0 auto;max-width:100%!important}</style>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="display:table;">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Serial_endosymbiosis.svg" title="An autogenous model for the origin of eukaryotes."><img alt="An autogenous model for the origin of eukaryotes." data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="607" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Serial_endosymbiosis.svg/420px-Serial_endosymbiosis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Serial_endosymbiosis.svg/630px-Serial_endosymbiosis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Serial_endosymbiosis.svg/840px-Serial_endosymbiosis.svg.png 2x" width="420"/></a><div class="thumbcaption" style="display:table-caption;caption-side:bottom;box-sizing:border-box;">An autogenous model for the origin of eukaryotes. </div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Autogenous models propose that a proto-eukaryotic cell containing a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> existed first, and later acquired <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latorre_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-latorre-135">[134]</a></sup> According to this model, a large <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryote</a> developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invaginations" title="Invaginations">invaginations</a> in its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">plasma membrane</a> in order to obtain enough <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_area_to_volume_ratio" title="Surface area to volume ratio">surface area</a> to service its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytoplasmic" title="Cytoplasmic">cytoplasmic</a> volume. As the invaginations differentiated in function, some became separate compartments – giving rise to the <a href="/wiki/Endomembrane_system" title="Endomembrane system">endomembrane system</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">golgi apparatus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_membrane" title="Nuclear membrane">nuclear membrane</a>, and single membrane structures such as <a href="/wiki/Lysosome" title="Lysosome">lysosomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">Mitochondria</a> are proposed to come from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiosis" title="Endosymbiosis">endosymbiosis</a> of an aerobic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteobacterium" title="Proteobacterium">proteobacterium</a>, and it is assumed that all the eukaryotic lineages that did not acquire mitochondria became extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> Chloroplasts came about from another endosymbiotic event involving <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>. Since all known eukaryotes have mitochondria, but not all have chloroplasts, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serial_endosymbiotic_theory" title="Serial endosymbiotic theory">serial endosymbiotic theory</a> proposes that mitochondria came first.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Chimeric_models">Chimeric models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chimeric models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chimera_(genetics)" title="Chimera (genetics)">Chimeric models</a> claim that two prokaryotic cells existed initially – an <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaeon</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterium</a>. The closest living relatives of these appears to be <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(archaea)" title="Asgard (archaea)">Asgardarchaeota</a> and (distantly related) the <a href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacteria" title="Alphaproteobacteria">alphaproteobacteria</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Proto-mitochondrion" title="Proto-mitochondrion">proto-mitochondrion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29929066_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29929066-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29695865_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29695865-139">[138]</a></sup> These cells underwent a merging process, either by a physical fusion or by <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiosis</a>, thereby leading to the formation of a eukaryotic cell. Within these chimeric models, some studies further claim that mitochondria originated from a bacterial ancestor while others emphasize the role of endosymbiotic processes behind the origin of mitochondria.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_inside-out_hypothesis">The inside-out hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: The inside-out hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The inside-out hypothesis suggests that the fusion between free-living mitochondria-like bacteria, and an archaeon into a eukaryotic cell happened gradually over a long period of time, instead of in a single <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis#In_protists" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytotic</a> event. In this scenario, an archaeon would trap aerobic bacteria with <a href="/wiki/Bleb_(cell_biology)" title="Bleb (cell biology)">cell protrusions</a>, and then keep them alive to draw energy from them instead of digesting them. During the early stages the bacteria would still be partly in direct contact with the environment, and the archaeon would not have to provide them with all the required nutrients. But eventually the archaeon would engulf the bacteria completely, creating the internal membrane structures and nucleus membrane in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25350791_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25350791-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is assumed the archaean group called <a href="/wiki/Halophile" title="Halophile">halophiles</a> went through a similar procedure, where they acquired as much as a thousand genes from a bacterium, way more than through the conventional <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a> that often occurs in the microbial world, but that the two microbes separated again before they had fused into a single eukaryote-like cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>An expanded version of the inside-out hypothesis proposes that the eukaryotic cell was created by physical interactions between two prokaryotic organisms and that the last common ancestor of eukaryotes got its genome from a whole population or community of microbes participating in cooperative relationships to thrive and survive in their environment. The genome from the various types of microbes would complement each other, and occasional horizontal gene transfer between them would be largely to their own benefit. This accumulation of beneficial genes gave rise to the genome of the eukaryotic cell, which contained all the genes required for independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCutcheon_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCutcheon-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callier_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callier-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_serial_endosymbiotic_hypothesis">The serial endosymbiotic hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: The serial endosymbiotic hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Symbiogenesis" title="Symbiogenesis">Symbiogenesis</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Symbiogenesis" title="Symbiogenesis">serial endosymbiotic theory</a> (championed by <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Margulis" title="Lynn Margulis">Lynn Margulis</a>), a union between a motile <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> bacterium (like <i>Spirochaeta</i>) and a thermoacidophilic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crenarchaeon" title="Crenarchaeon">crenarchaeon</a> (like <i>Thermoplasma</i> which is sulfidogenic in nature) gave rise to the present day eukaryotes. This union established a motile organism capable of living in the already existing acidic and sulfurous waters. Oxygen is known to cause toxicity to organisms that lack the required <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> machinery. Thus, the archaeon provided the bacterium with a highly beneficial reduced environment (sulfur and sulfate were reduced to sulfide). In microaerophilic conditions, oxygen was reduced to water thereby creating a mutual benefit platform. The bacterium on the other hand, contributed the necessary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> products and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> acceptors along with its motility feature to the archaeon thereby gaining a swimming <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motility</a> for the organism.
</p><p>From a consortium of bacterial and archaeal DNA originated the nuclear <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of eukaryotic cells. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirochete" title="Spirochete">Spirochetes</a> gave rise to the motile features of eukaryotic cells. Endosymbiotic unifications of the ancestors of alphaproteobacteria and cyanobacteria, led to the origin of mitochondria and <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a> respectively. For example, <i>Thiodendron</i> has been known to have originated via an <a href="/wiki/Ectosymbiosis" title="Ectosymbiosis">ectosymbiotic</a> process based on a similar <a href="/wiki/Syntrophy" title="Syntrophy">syntrophy</a> of sulfur existing between the two types of bacteria – <i><a href="/wiki/Desulfobacter" title="Desulfobacter">Desulfobacter</a></i> and <i>Spirochaeta</i>.
</p><p>However, such an association based on motile symbiosis has never been observed practically. Also there is no evidence of archaeans and spirochetes adapting to intense acid-based environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latorre_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-latorre-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_hydrogen_hypothesis">The hydrogen hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The hydrogen hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_hypothesis" title="Hydrogen hypothesis">hydrogen hypothesis</a>, the symbiotic linkage of an anaerobic and <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">autotrophic</a> <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> archaeon (host) with an alphaproteobacterium (the symbiont) gave rise to the eukaryotes. The host utilized <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (H<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub>) to produce <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> while the symbiont, capable of aerobic respiration, expelled H<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> as byproducts of anaerobic fermentation process. The host's methanogenic environment worked as a sink for H<sub>2</sub>, which resulted in heightened bacterial fermentation.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_gene_transfer" title="Endosymbiotic gene transfer">Endosymbiotic gene transfer</a> acted as a catalyst for the host to acquire the symbionts' <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> metabolism and turn <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a> in nature. Subsequently, the host's methane forming capability was lost. Thus, the origins of the heterotrophic organelle (symbiont) are identical to the origins of the eukaryotic <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineage</a>. In this hypothesis, the presence of H<sub>2</sub> represents the selective force that forged eukaryotes out of prokaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_syntrophy_hypothesis">The syntrophy hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: The syntrophy hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Syntrophy" title="Syntrophy">syntrophy</a> hypothesis was developed in contrast to the hydrogen hypothesis and proposes the existence of two symbiotic events. According to this model, the origin of eukaryotic cells was based on metabolic symbiosis (syntrophy) between a methanogenic archaeon and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deltaproteobacterium" title="Deltaproteobacterium">deltaproteobacterium</a>. This syntrophic symbiosis was initially facilitated by H<sub>2</sub> transfer between different species under anaerobic environments. In earlier stages, an alphaproteobacterium became a member of this integration, and later developed into the mitochondrion. <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">Gene</a> transfer from a deltaproteobacterium to an archaeon led to the methanogenic archaeon developing into a nucleus. The archaeon constituted the genetic apparatus, while the deltaproteobacterium contributed towards the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasmic</a> features.
</p><p>This theory incorporates two selective forces at the time of nucleus <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>
</p>
<ul><li>presence of metabolic partitioning to avoid the harmful effects of the co-existence of <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolic</a> cellular pathways, and</li>
<li>prevention of abnormal <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein biosynthesis</a> due to a vast spread of <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a> in the archaeal genes after acquiring the mitochondrion and losing <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">methanogenesis</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<h5><span id="6.2B_serial_endosymbiosis_scenario"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="6+_serial_endosymbiosis_scenario">6+ serial endosymbiosis scenario</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eukaryote&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 6+ serial endosymbiosis scenario">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>A complex scenario of 6+ serial endosymbiotic events of archaea and bacteria has been proposed in which mitochondria and an asgard related archaeota were acquired at a late stage of eukaryogenesis, possibly in combination, as a secondary endosymbiont.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> The findings have been rebuked as an artifact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
